[2025-11-12T02:18:34Z] Pavan Pamidimarri: This painting covers the back wall of the refectory of the Convent of Santa Maria delle Grazie (Holy Mary of Grace) in Milan. It was painted between the years 1495 and 1498, and depicts a scene from the Gospel of John, 13:21. The painting started to flake within 20 years, and by the time it was 70 years old, it was described as “ruined” with subjects unrecognizable. In 1652, a doorway was cut right through the unrecognizable picture, and then bricked up. The painting was vandalized by anti-clerical French revolutionary troops in 1796. The refectory was bombed during World War II. The latest restoration effort took 21 years and the mural opened to the public in 1999. Name the mural and the painter.

[2025-11-12T05:46:02Z] మాन$: Harley-Davidson motorcycles are called "Hogs" due to a 1920s racing team that had a piglet as a mascot, which the team would carry on victory laps. After a win, journalists dubbed them "the Harley Hogs," suggesting they were "hogging" all the victories from other teams. The nickname eventually became synonymous with the brand, and the company later adopted "H.O.G." for the Harley Owners Group.

[2025-11-12T10:13:03Z] Ryan Colaço: Don’t have time to type the entire story…so, here’s a GPT version (verified factual) ↵ 1. The context: A deadly mystery ↵ In the mid-19th century, London was repeatedly struck by outbreaks of cholera, a disease that caused severe diarrhea and dehydration, killing thousands. ↵ At the time, the dominant theory was miasma — the belief that diseases spread through “bad air” or foul smells from rotting matter. ↵ Snow, an anesthesiologist by training (he famously gave chloroform to Queen Victoria during childbirth), doubted this. He suspected that cholera was waterborne, spread through contaminated drinking water. But he needed proof. ↵ 2. Early clues: The Soho outbreak (1854) ↵ In August 1854, a major cholera outbreak erupted in London’s Soho district, killing more than 600 people within a few weeks. Snow saw this as an opportunity to test his theory. ↵ He went door-to-door, interviewing residents about where they got their drinking water and mapping cholera cases. This was unusual — a mix of social investigation and data science before either field existed. ↵ 3. The Broad Street pump ↵ When Snow plotted the cases on a map, a clear pattern emerged: ↵ Most deaths clustered around the Broad Street water pump (now Broadwick Street). ↵ Yet some puzzling exceptions strengthened his case: ↵ Workers at a nearby brewery stayed healthy because they drank beer instead of water. ↵ A woman who lived far away died after having water from Broad Street delivered to her because she liked its taste. ↵ Snow convinced the local authorities to remove the handle of the Broad Street pump — and the outbreak soon subsided. ↵ 4. The second line of evidence: Water companies ↵ To reinforce his theory, Snow also studied two London water companies: ↵ The Southwark and Vauxhall Company drew water from a polluted part of the Thames. ↵ The Lambeth Company had moved its intake upriver to cleaner water. ↵ Houses supplied by the polluted company had far higher cholera death rates — even when they were on the same street as those using clean water. ↵ This provided strong, population-level evidence that cholera was transmitted through contaminated water, not air. ↵ 5. The aftermath ↵ Although Snow’s findings were initially dismissed — the miasma theory was deeply entrenched — his meticulous data collection and mapping later became cornerstones of epidemiology and public health. ↵ His Broad Street map is now legendary as one of the first examples of data visualization used to solve a public health crisis.
